A TALK as woman to woman, in which each shall say, 
" 1 " and " you," and " my dear," and " you know," as free- 
ly as she pleases. It would not be a womanly chat if we 
omitted these forms of expression. An informal jirefacti to 
what I mean shall be an informal book — bijstJing with 
" I's " all the way through. If said bristles offond the 
r.ritic's touch, let him remember that this work is not pre- 
pared for the library, but for readers who trouble themselves 
little about editorial "we's"and the circumlocutions c: 
literary modesty. 

1 wish it were in my power to bring you, the prospec- 
tive owner of this volume, in person, as I do in spirit, to 
my side on this winter evening, when the bairnies are 
" folded like the flocks ; " the orders for breakfast com- 
mitted to the keeping of Bridget, or Gretchen, or Chloe, oi 
the plans for the morrow definitely laid in the bi-ain of that 
e^er-busy, but most independent of women, the housekeep- 
er who " does her own wor'k." I should perhaps summon te 
our cozy conference a very weary companion— weary of 
foot, of hand — and I should not deserve to be your confidant, 
did 1 not know how often heai-t-weary with discouragement ; 
with much producing of ways and means ; with a certain 
deispondent looking forward to the monotonous grinding of 
Jie household machine; to the certainty, proved by j)aKt ex

perience, that toilsome as lias been this day, the ocrro'w 
will prove yet more abundant in labors, in trials of strength, 
and nerves, and tempf^r. You would tell me what a dreary 
problem this of " woman's work that is never done " is to 
f«ur fainting soul how, try as you may and as you do to

up " in the treadmill to keep you on the sti'ain ; how you - 
often say to yourself, in bitterness of spii'it, that it is a mis-

of scholastic training will make you a better cook, a better 
wife or mother. You have seen the time — nay, many times 
since assuming your present position — when you would have 
exchanged your knowledge of ancient and modern languages 
belles-lettres, music, and -natural science, for the skill of a

mature housewives, unsoftened by sympathy even from your 
husband, or your father or brother, or whoever may be the 
" one " to whom you " make home lovely." It may be that, 
In utter discouragement, you have made up your mind that 
you have " no talent for these things." 

I have before me now the picture of a wife, the mother 
of four children, who, many years ago, sickened me for all 
time with that phrase. In a slatternly morning-gown at 
four in the afternoon, leaning back in the laziest and most 
ragged of rocking-chairs, dust on the carpet, on the open 
piano, the mantel, the miri'ors, even on her own hai", she 
rubbed the soft palm of one haiid with the grimy fingers of 
the other, and with a sickly-sweet smile whined out — 

" Now, I am one of the kind who have no talent foi 
luch things ! The kitchen and house vvork and rsewiug are 
absolutely liatcfiJ to me — utterly uncongenial to my turn

of uiind. The height of my earthly ambition is to have 
nolhiug to do but to paint on velvet all day ! " 

1 felt then, in the height of my indignant disgust, that 
there was propriety as well as wit in the " Spectator's " sug- 
gestion that every young woman should, before fijdng the 
wedding-day, be compelled by law to exhibit to inspectors a

bed-linen, clothing, etc., made by her own hands, and that 
it would be wise legislation which should add to these proofs

housework and cookery. 

If you have not what our Yankee grandmothers termed 
a " faculty " for housewifely — yet are obliged, as is the 
case with an immense majority of American women, to 
conduct the afiairs of a household, bills of fare included — 
there is tlie more reason for earnest application to your pro- 
fession. If the natural taste be dull, lay to it more strength 
of will — resolution born of a j ust sense of the importance 
of the knowledge and dexterity you would acquire. Do 
not scoff at the woi'd " ])rofession." Call not that commor 
and unclean which l^rovideuce- has designated as your life- 
work. I .speak ]iot now of the labors of the culinary de- 
paitruent alone ; but, without naming the other duties which 
voii and you only can perform, I do insist that upon method, 
skill, economy in the kitchen, depends so much of the well- 
being of the rest of the household, that it may safely be 
styled the root — the foundation of house\>ifery. I own it 
wovid be plea.santer in most cases, especially to those who 
have cultivated a taste for intellectual pursuits, to live 
above the heat and odor of this department. It must be 
very fine to have an efficient aide-de-camp in the person of 
ft French cook, or a competent sub-manager, or an accom- 
plished head-waiter who receives your orders for the day in 
Four boudoir or library, and executes the same with zca!

Before undertaking the preparation of any dish, read ovei

with the manufaeturfc of it. Many excellent housewives 
have a fashion of saying loftily, when asked how such things

wrote out one in my life. " 

And you, if timid and self-distrustful, ai'e smitten with 
shame, keep yo\ir receipt-book out of sight, and cram your 
memory with ingredients and measures, times and weights, 
for fear Mrs. Notable should susj)ect you of rawness and 
anefiiciency. Whereas the truth is, that if you liavoaniind 
RTorthy of the name, its i)owers are too valuable to be laden 
«d.th such details. Master the general principles, as I said 
just now, and for particulars look to your marching-orders. 
Having refreshed your memory by this reference, ])ick out 
from your household .stores, and set in convenient order, 
within reach of your baud, everything you will need in 
maJ^ing ready the particidar comi)ouud under consideration. 
Then take j'our stand in the midst — or sit, if you can. It 
is common sense — oftentimes a jiious duty, to take judicious 
.Tare of your physical health. I lay it down as a safe and im 
pp.rative rule for kitchen use — Never stand when you can

Next, j)re{jare each ingredient for mixing, thai the broad,

" Dickens again ? '■ 

Why not, since there is no more genial and pertinenl 
philosopher of common life and every-day subjects ? To 
quote, thou : — 

"It was a maxim of Captain Swosser's," said Mrs, 
Badger, " speaking in his figurative, naval manner, that 
when you make pitch hot, you cannot make it too hot, and 
that If you have only to swab a plank, you should swab it 
as if Davy Jones were after you. It appears to me that this 
maxim is appKcable to the medical as well as the nautical 
profession." 

" To all professions 1 " observed Mr. Badger. " It was 
admirably said by Captain Swossor ; beautifully said ! " 

But it will sometimes happen that when you have heated 
your pitch, or swabbed your deck, or made your pudding 
accoi'ding to the lights set before you, the result is a failure. 
This is especially apt to occur in a maiden effort. You 
have wasted materials and time, and suffered, moi-eover, 
acut« demoializatiou — are enwrapped in a wet blanket ol 
discouragement, instead of the seemly robe of coraplaoency. 
Yet no part of the culinary education is more useful, if 
turned to proper account, than this V3ry discipline of failure. 
It is a stepping- stone to excellence — sharp, it is true, bui 
often sure. You have learned how not to do it right, vvhiofc 
is the next thing to siiccess. It is pretty certain that yoi; 
will avoid, in your second essay, the rock 'ipon which you 
have split this tijxe. And, after all, there are few failunn 



FAMILIAE TALK WITH MY KEADEE. iiO 

wMch are utter and irremediable. Scorched soups ami 
custards, sour bread, biscuit yellow with soda, and caku 
heavy as lead, come under the head of " hopeless." Thej 
MB absolutely unfit to be set before civilized beings and 
educated stomachs. Shoidd such mishaps occur, lock the 
memory of the attempt in your own bosom, and do not vex 
or amuse John and your guests with the narration, stiU lesa 
with visible proof of the calamity. Many a partial failure 
would pass unobserved but for the clouded brow and earnest 
apologies of the hostess. Do not apologize except at the 
last gasp ! If there is but one chance in ten that a single 
person present may not discover the deficiency which has 
changed all food on the table to dust and gravel-stones ro 
you, trust to the one chance, and carry jfi' the mattar 
bravely. You ^v^ll be astonished to find, if you keep your 
wits about you how often even your husband will remain 
in blissful ignorance that aught has gone wrong, if you do 
not tell him. You know ro v/ell what should have been the 
product of your labor that you exaggerate the justice of 
others' perceptions. Console yourself, furthermore, with 
the reflection that yours is not the first failure upon record, 
nor the million-and-first, and that there wiU be as many to- 
morrows as there have been yesterdays. 

Don't add to a trifling contretemps the real discomfort 
of a discontented or fretful wife. Say blithely, if John 
not* your misfortune—" I hope to do better another time," 
and do not be satisfied until you have redeemed youi; 
pledge. Experience and your quick wit will soon teach you 
how to avert impending evils of this nature, how to snatch 
your preparations from immiuent destruction, and, by in- 
genious corre stives or concealments, to make them present- 
able, lliese you wiU soon learn for yourself if you keep 
before you the truism I have already written, to wit, th&i 
few failures are beyond repair.

Never try experiments for the benefit of invited gueBts 
nor, when John is at home, risk the success of your meai 
upon a new dish. Have something wliich you know he 
can eat, and introduce experiments as by-play. But do not 
be too sliy of innovations in the sliape of untried dishes. 
Variety is not only pleasant, but healthful. The leas*, 
pampered palate will weary of stereotyped bills of fare. It 
Lg an idea which should have been exploded long ago, that 
plain roast, boiled, and fried, on Monday, Tuesday, Wednes- 
day, and Thursday, cod-fish on Friday, with pork-and-beans 
every Saturday, are means of grace, because economical 
And with this should have vanished the prejudice against 
warmed-over meals — or rechauffes, as our French friends 
term them. I have tried, in the following pages, to set 
forth the attractions of these, their claims to your attention 
as being savory, economical, nourishing, and often elegant. 
In preparing these acceptably, everything depends upon 
your own taste and skill. Season with judgment, cook 
just enough and not a minute too long, and dish nicely. 
The recommendation of the eye to the palate is a point no 
cook can aflbrd to disregard. If you can offer an unex- 
pected visitor notliing better than bread-and-butter and cold 
ham, he will enjoy the luncheon twice as much if the bread 
be sliced thinly and evenly, spread smoothly, each slice 
folded in the middle upon the buttered surface, and piled 
symmetrically ; if the ham be also cut tlvin, scarcely thicker 
than a wafer, and garnished with parsley, cresses, or curled 
lettuce. Set on mustard and pickles ; let the table-cloth 
and napkin be white and glossy ; the glass clear, and plate 
shining clean ; and add to these accessories to comfort a 
bright welcome, and, my word for it, you need fear no dis- 
satisfaction on his part, however epicurean may be hi* 
testes. Should your cupboard be bare of aught more sub 
stAiitial than crackers and cheese, do not yiel 1 to disr»ay

.i[)lit the crackers (if siilittable), toast the inside liglitly, 
Slid butter while hot. Grate your cheese into a i)Owdery 
mound, garnishing the edges of the plate. If you have no 
beverage except water to set before him, let this be cool, 
Bud pour it out for him yourself, into an irreproachable 
glass. A dirty table-cloth, a smeared goblet, or a sticky 
plate, will spoil the most luxurious feast. A table well set 
is half-spread. 

I have not said one-tentb of that which is pressing upon 
my heart and mind, yet 1 fear you may think me trite and 
tedious. One suggestion more, and we will proceed to the 
details of business. 

I believe that, so far as care can avail in securing sunh 
H res^ilt, my receipts are accurate. But in the matter of 
Nfcasofiing and other minor details, consult your judgment 
and John's taste. Take this liberty with whatever receipt 
fou think you can improve. If I chance to find in youi 
work-basket, or upon the kitchen dresser, a well-thimibec 
copy of my beloved " Common Sense," with copious anno 
tations in the margin, I shall, so far from feeling wounded, 
be flatt'jea in having so diligent a student, and, with yoni 
permif.; ion, shall engraft the most happy suggestions upon 
the second edition.

SOUPis 

The case of yoitr soup should always b'j mcooked meai 
To this may be added, if you like, cracked bones of cooked 
game, or of underdone beef or mutton ; but for flavor and 
nourishment, depend upon the juices of the meat which 
was put in raw. Cut this into small pieces, and beat the 
bone until it is fractured at every inch of its length. Put 
Ihem on in cold water, without salt, and heat very slowly. 
Do not boil fast at any stage of the operation. Keep the 
pot covered, and do not add the salt until the meat is thor- 
oughly done, as it has a tendency to harden the fibres, and 
restrain the flow of the juices. Strain — always through a 
cullender, after which clear soups should be filtered througi- 
a hair-sieve or coarse bobbinet lace. The bag should not. 
be squeezed. 

It is slovenly to leave rags of meat, husks of vegetables 
and bits of bone in the tureen. Do not uncover until you 
are ready to ladle out the soup. Do this neatly and quickly, 
having your soup-plates heated beforehand. 

Most soups are better the second day than the first, un- 
less they are warmed over too quickly or left too long upon 
the fire after they are hot. In the one case they are apt 
to scorch; in the other they become insipid.

Green Pea. (No. !.)»}« 

4 lbs. beef — cut into small pieces. 
^ jjeck of green peas. 

1 gallon water. 

Boil the empty pods of the peas in the water one hoijt 
before putting in the beef. Strain them oait, add the beef, 
and boil slowly for an hour and a half longer. Half an hour 
before serving, add the shelled peas ; and twenty minutes 
later, half a cup of rice-flour, with salt and pepper. A 
little chopped parsley is an improvement. After adding 
the rice-flour, stir frequently, to prevent scorching. Strain 
Into a hot tureen.

Pea and Tomato. »J« 

This is made according to either of the foregoing receipts, 
in summer with green — in winter with dried and split 
peas. Just before straining the soup, add a quart of toma- 
toes, which have already been stewed soft ; let the whole

Bean (dried). 4* 

The beans used for this purpose may be the ordinary 
kidney, the rice or field bean, or, best of all, the French 
mock-turtle soup bean. Soak a quart of these over night 
in soft lukewarm water ; put them over the fire next morn- 
ing, with one gallon of cold water and about two pounds of 
salt pork. Boil slowly for three hours, keeping the pot 
well covered ; shred into it a head of celery, add pepper — 
cayenne, if preferred — simmer half an hour longer, strain 
through a cullender, and serve, with slices of lemon passed 
to each guest. 

Mock-turtle beans, treated in this way, yield a very fail 
gubetitute for the fine calf 's-head soup known by the same 
oame.

n lis is a ■winter soup, aad is made of white beans pr&

used — and the Shaker sweet com is nearly as good for the

the bean soup proper. Strain out as usual. Some persona 
have a habit of neglecting the use of the cullender in mak- 
ing bean soup, and serving it like stewed beans which have 
been imperfectly drained. The practice is both slovenly 
and unwholesome, since the husks of the cereal are thus im- 
posed upon the digestive organs of the eater, with no addi- 
tional nutriment. To the beans and com may be added a 
pint of stewed tomato, if desired.

MEAT SO TIPS. 
Beef Soup (a la Jkdienne). ^ 
6 lbs. of lean beef. The shin is a good piece for this pur- 
pose. Have the bones well cracked, carefully extracting 
the marrow, every bit of which should be put into the 
soup. 
6 qts. of water. 

The stock must be prepared the day before the soup is 
needed. Put the beef, bones and all, with the water in a 
close vessel, and set it where it will heat gradually. Let it 
boil very slowly for six hours at least, only uncovering the 
pot once in a great while to see if there is danger of the 
water sinking too rapidly. Should this be the case, replen- 
ish with boiling water, taking care not to put in too much. 
During the seventh hour, take off the soup and set it away, 
still closely covered, until next morning. About an hour 
before dinner, take out the meat, which you can use for 
mince-meat, if you wish ; remove the cake of fat from the 
surface of the stock, set the soup over the fire, and throw in 
a little salt to bring up the scum. When this has been 
tkiramed carefully off, put in your vegetables. These should 
be-- - 

2 carrots. 

3 turnips. 

Half a head of white cabbage. 

1 pt. green corn — or dried Shaker com, Boaked 

over night. 
1 head celery. 
1 qt. ioxaaiaea. 



86 COMMON SENSE. 

These slioiilJ be prepared for the soup by slicing them 
very small, and stewing them in barely enoiigh water to 
cover them, until they break to pieces. Cook the cabbage 
by itself in two waters — throwing the first away. The onlj 
exception to the general dissolution, is in the case of a single 
carrot, which should likewise be cooked alone and whole, 
untU thoroughly done, and set aside to cool, when the rest 
of the vegetables, with the water in which they were boiled, 
are added to the spup. Return the pot to the tire with the 
vegetables and stock, and boil slowly for half an hour from 
the time ebullition actually begins. Strain without pressing, 
only shaking and lightly stirring the contents of the cul- 
lender. The vegetables having been added with all theii 
juices already cooked, much boiling and squeezing are not 
needed, and only make the soup cloudy. Cut the reserved 
carrot into dice and drop into the clear liquor after it is in 
the tureen, — also, if you like, a handful of a ermiceUi, or 
jiacaroni which has been boiled tender in clear water. 

The seasoning of this excellent soup is a matter of taste. 
Some use only salt and white pepper. Others like witli 
this a few blades of mace, and boil in the stock a handful 
of sweet herbs. And others fancy that, in addition to 
these, a glass of brown sherry imparts a flavor that renders 
it peculiarly acceptable to most palates. Send to table very 
hot, and have the soup-plates likewise heated.

MuTTOjf OB Lamb Brotu. »J« 

4 lbs. mutton or lamb — lean — cut into small pieces. 
1 gallon water. 
^ teao ipful rice. 

Boil tl.e unsuilted meat for two Lours, slowly, in a coyered 
vessel. Soak the rice in enough warm water to cover it, 
md at the end of this time idd it, water and all, to the boil- 
ing soup. Cook an hour longer, stirring watchfully from 
time to time, lest the rice should settle and adhere to the 
bottom of the pot. Beat an egg to a froth and stir into a 
3up of cold milk, into which has been rubbed smoothly a 
tablespoonful rice or wheat flour. Mix with this, a little at 
at a time, some of the scalding liquor, vmtU the egg is so fai 
cooked that there is no danger of curdling in the soup. 
Pour into the pot, when you have taken out the meat, 
season with parsley, thyme, pepper, and salt. Boil up fairly, 
and serve. If allowed to stand on the fire, it is apt to bum. 

This soup may be made from the liquor in which a leg 
of mutton has been boiled, provided too much salt was not 
put in with it. It is especially good when the stock is 
chicken broth. For the sick it is palatable and nutritious 
with the rice left in. When strained it makes a nice white 
table soup, and is usually relished by all.

In all receipts in which ham is mentioned as seasoning, 
reference is made to corned, not smoked pork. The smoke 
imparts an undisguisab'le, and, to many, an unpleasant 
flavor, especially to delicate soups and ragouts. 

Mock-Turtle or Calf's Head Soup. •J^ 

1 large calf's head, well cleaned and washed. 
4 pig's feet, « " « « 

This soup should always be prepared the day before it 
is to be served up. Lay the head and feet in the bottom of 
a large pot, and cover with a gallon of water. Let it boil 
"ihree hours, or until the flesh will slip easily from the 
bones. Take out the head, leaving in the- feet, and allow 
these to boil steadily while you cut the meat from the 
head. Select with care enough of the fatty portions which 
lie on the top of the head and the cheeks to fill a tea-cup, 
and set them aside to cool. Remove the brains to a saucer 
and also set aside. Chop the rest ■ of the meat with the 
tongue very fine, season with salt, pepper, powdered mar- 
joram and thyme, a teaspoonful of cloves, the same of mace, 
half as much allspice, and a grated nutmeg, and return to 
the pot. When the flesh falls from the bones of the pig's 
feet, take o-it the latter, leaving in the gelatinous meat, 
Let all boil together slowly, without removing the cover 
for two hours more ; take the soup from the fii-e and set H 
a'way until the next day. An hour before dinner, sot on 



iO COMMON SENSE. 

the stock to warm. Wlien it boils strain carefully, and 
drop in the meat you have reserved, which, when cold, 
should be cut into small squares. Have these all ready ax 
well as the force-meat balls. To prepare these, rub the 
yolks of five hard-boiled eggs to a paste in a Wedgewood 
mortar, or in a bowl, with the back of a silver tablespoon, 
adding gradually the brains to moisten them, also a little 
butter and salt. Mix with these two eggs beaten very 
light, flour your bands, and make this paste into balls about 
the size of a pigeon's egg. Throw them into the soup five 
minutes before you take it off the fire ; Stir in a largv' 
tablespoonful of browned flour rubbed smooth in a little 
cold water, let it boil up, and finish the seasoning by tho 
addition of a glass and a half of good wine — sherry or Ma • 
deira — and the juice of a lemon. It shoiild not boil morn 
than half an hour on the second day. Serve with slicec\ 
lemon. Some lay the slices upon the top of the soup, 
but the better plan is to pass to the guests a smiil dish 
containing these. 

This has been well called the " king of soups," and ia 
actually more delicious than the real turtle soup. It is 
hoped no one will be afraid to undertake the preparation 
of it on account of the apparently tedious and delicate mode 
I have described. If the directions be closely followed, the 
result is sure to be satisfactory, and the task is really much 
less troublesome than it appears to be.

Veal and Saoo Soup. 

2} lbs. veal. 

J- lb. pearl sago. 

1 pt. mUk. 

4 eggs. 

3 qts. water. 

Put on the veal and water — the meat chopped finely — 
and boil for two hours until the liquid is reduced to abou< 
one-half the original quantity. Strain out the shreds of 
meat through a coarse cloth, and put the soup again over 
the fire. Meanwhile the sago should be washed in several 
waters, and soaked half an hour in warm water enough to 
cover it. Stir it into the strained broth and boil — stirring 
very often to prevent lumping or scorching — ^half an hour 
more. Heat the milk almost to boiling ; beat the yolks of 
the eggs very light ; mix with the milk gradually, as in 
making boiled custard, and pom" — stiixing all the while — 
into the soup. Season with pepper and salt ; boil up once 
to cook the eggs, and serve. Should the liquid be too thick 
after putting in the sago, replenish with boiling water. It 
should be about the consistency of hot custard. 

Tliis soup is very good, if chicken broth be substituted 
for the veal. It is very strengthening to invalids, and es- 
pecially beneficial to those suffering with colds or pulmontuy 
affections.

Hare or Kabbit Soup, 

Dissect the rabbit, crack the bones, and prepare precisely 
as you would the venison soup, only putting in three smaU 
onions instead of one, and a bunch of sweet herbs. Hares 
which are too tough to be cooked in any other way, make 
excellent game soup. Also, the large gray squirrel of tha 
Middle and Southern States. 

I have eaten squirrel soup that was really delicious.

Otsteu Sou5/(No. 1). »{• 

2 qts. of oysters. 

1 qt. of milk. 

^ tablespoonfuls butter. 

1 teacupful water. 

Strain tlie liquor from the oysters, add to it the w-atei, 
Mi>1 sot it over the fire to heat slowly in a covered vessel. 
When it is near boiling, season with pej/per and salt, and 
stir in the mUk, after which stir constantly, unless, as is 
wisest, you heat the liquor in a vessel set in a pot of boil- 
tag water. When the soup again nears the boiling-point, 
add the oysters, and let them stew until they " ruffle " on 
the edge. This will be in about five minutes. Then put in 
the butter and stir well until it is melted, when the soup ia 
ready for use. 

Serve with sliced lemon and oyster or cream crackers. 
Some use mace and nutmeg in seasoning. The crowmng 
excellence in oyster soup is to have it cooked just enough. 
Too much stewing ruins the bivalves, while an underdone 
oyster is a flabby abomination. The plumpness of the main 
body and ruffled edge are good indices of their right con- 
dition.

Cat-fish Soup. 4« 

Few persons are aware into what a variety of tempting 
dishes this much-abused fish can be made. Those who 
have only seen the bloated, unsightly creatures that play 
the scavengers about city wharves, are excusable for enter- 
taining a prejudice against them as an article of food. But 
the small cat-fish of our inland lakes and streams are alto- 
gether respectable, except in their unfortunate name. 

6 cat-fish, in average weight half a pound apiece. 
J lb. salt pork. 

1 pint milk. 

2 eggs. 

1 head of celery, or a small bag of celery-seed. 

Skin and clean the fish and cut them up. Choj) the 
pork into small pieces. Put these together into the pot, 
with two quarts of water, chopped sweet herbs, and the 
celery seasoning. Boil for an hour, or until fish and porl- 
are in rags, and strain, if you desire a regular soup for & 
first course. Return to the saucepan and add the mUk, 
which should be already hot. Next the eggs, beaten to a 
froth, and a hunp of butter the size of a walnut. Boil up 
once, and serve with dice of toasted bread on the top. 
Pass sliced lemon, or walnut or butternut pickles with it. 

Eel Soup 

Eel soup is made in precisely the same manner as cat- 
fish, only boiled longer. A chopped onion is no detriment 
t<> the flavor of either, and will removo the muddy taste 
which these fish sometimes acquire from turbid strewT'fj.

LoBSTEB Soup. 

2 qta. veal or chicken broth, -well strained. 

1 large lobster. 

2 eggs — boiled hard 

Boi! the lobster and extract the meat, setting aside th. 
ooral in a cool place. Cut or chop up the meat found in 
the d»ws. Eub the yolks of the eggs to a paste with a 
teaspoonfuL of butter, Pound and rub the claw-meat in 
the same manner, and Tniy with the yolka Beat up a raw 
egg, and stir into the paste ; season with pepper, salt, and, 
if you like, mace ; make into force-meat balls, and set away 
with the coral to cool and harden. By this time the stock 
should be well heated, when put in the rest of the lobster 
meat cut into square bits. BoU fifteen minutes, which time 
employ in pounding the coral in a Wedgewood mortar, or 
earthenware bowl, i-ubbing it into a fine, even paste, with 
the addition of a few spoonfuls of the broth, gradually 
worked in until it is about the consistency of boiled starch. 
Stir very carefully into the hot soup, which should, in the 
process, blush into a roseate hue. Lastly, drop in the 
force-meat balls, after which do not stir, lest they should 
break. Simmer a few minutes to cook the raw egg ; but, 
if allowed to boil, the soup will darken. 

Crab soup may be made in the same way, excepting tiu 
OoiaUine process, crabs being destitute of that dainty.

Boiled Codfish. {Fresh.) ^ 

Lay the fish in cold water, slightly salted, foi half aa 
hour before it is time to coot it. Wipe it dry and put it 
iuto the fish-kettle with water enough to co-ver it, in wliich 
Las been dissolved a little salt. Let it boil quite briskly. 
A piece of cod weighing three pounds will be cooked in half 
an hour from the time the water fairly boUs, if put in without 
a cloth. But by far the better plan is, when the cod haa 
been wiped free of the salt and water, to wrap it in a clean 
Unen cloth kept for such purposes. , The cloth should bo 
dredged with flour, to prevent sticking. Sew up the edges in 
such a manner as to envelop the fish entirely, yet have but 
one thickness of the cloth over any part. The wrapping 
should be fitted neatly to the shape of the piece to be cook- 
ed. Fish cooked thus wUl require twice as long to boil aa 
when put into the water without such protection ; but the 
flavor is better preserved, and when unwrapped, it will not 
present the sodden appearance and crumbling grain that 
disfigure most boUed fish. 

Have ready a sauce prepared thus : — 

To one giU boiling water add aa much milk, and when it is 
Bcalding-hot, stir in -leaving the sauce-pan on the fire — ^two 
tablespoonfuls of butter, a little at a time, that it may melt 
without oiling, a tablespoonful of flour previously wet with 
cold water, and, as this thickens, two beaten eggs. Season 
with salt and chopped parsley, and when, after one good boU, 
you withdraw it from the fii-e, add a dozen capers, or pickled 
nasturtium seeds, or, if you prefer, a spoonful of vinegar in 
which celery-seeds have been steeped. Put the fish into a 
hot dish, and pour the sauce over it. Some serve in a but- 
ter-boat ; but I fancy that the boiling sauce applied to tha 
rteaming fish imparts a richness it cannot gain later. Gar

Boiled Codfish. {Salt.) 

Put tlie fisli to soak over night in lukewarm water — at 
early as eight o'clock in the evening. Change this for more 
warm water at bed-time and cover closely. Change again 
in the morning and wash off the salt. Two hours before 
dinner take out the cod, examine to see that no crystals of 
salt adhere to the under part, and plunge into very cold 
watei'. This makes it firm. Finally, set over the fire witii 
enough lukewarm water to cover it, and boil for half an 
hour. Draki well ; lay it in a hot dish, and poiir over it 
egg-sauce prepared as in the foregoing receipt, only substi 
tuting the yolks of two hard-boiled eggs, rubbed to a paste 
with butter, for the beaten raw egg.

Pickled Salmon. {Fresh.) •{• 
Having ileaned your fish, cut into pieces of a con 
eenient size to go into the fish-kettle, and boil in salted 
water as for the table. Drain it very dry, wipe it with a 
clean cloth, and set it aside until next morning. 

Make pickle enough to cover it in the following propop 
tions : 2 quarts vinegar, a dozen blades of mace, dozen 
white peppers, dozen cloves, two teaspoonfuls made mus- 
tard, three tablespoonfuls white sugar, and a pint of the 
water ia which the fish was boiled. Let them boil up once 
hard, that you may skim the pickle. Should the spicej 
come away with the scum ia large quantities, pick them 
out and return to the kettle. Set the liquor away in an 
parthenware jar, closely covered to keep in the flavor. 
Next morning hang it over a brisk fire in a bell-metal 
Vettle (covered), and heat to boiling. Meanwhile, prepare 
/he salmon by cutting into pieces an inch and a half long 
imd haK an inch wide. Out cleanly and regularly with a 
Bbarp knife. When they are aU ready, and the liquor is on 
the boil, drop them carefuUy into the kettle. Let the 
pickle boil up once to make sure the sahnon is heated 
through. Have ready some air-tight glass jars, such as 
you iise for canning fruit and tomatoes. Take the salmon 
from the kettle, while it is stiU on the stove or range, with 
a wire egg-beater, taking care you do not break the pieces. 
Drop them rapidly into the jar, packing closely as you go 
on ; fiU with the boiling pickle until it overflows, screw on 
the top, and set away in a dark, cool place. Proceed in 
tlie same way with each can until all are full. Salmon 
thus put up will keep good for years, as I can testify from 
experience, and will weU repay the trouble of preparation. 
YoM can vary the seasoning to your tas'te, addiag a shallot 
ar two minced veiy fine, some celery and small pods of 
^.yenne pepper, which always looh well in vinegar. 



FISB. 59 

Be su?e that the contents of the kettle are boiling when 
Iransferred to the cans, that they are not allowed time tc 
cool in the transit, that the elastic on the can is properly 
adjusted, and the top screwed down tightly, and success it 
certain. I would call the attention of those who are fond 
of the potted spiced salmon, sold at a high price in gro 
eery-stores, to this receipt for making the same luxury at 
home. It costs less by one-half, is as good, and is always 
on hand. 

Pickled Salmon. (Scdt.) 

At certain seasons of the year fresh salmon cannot ba 
procured, even by the dwellers in cities, while those who live 
jn the country sometimes do not see it from one year's end 
uo the other. But dried salmon can always be had in any 
tolerably well-kept grocery, and a very nice relish prepared 
trom it. 

"Wash the salmon in two or three waters, rubbing it 
lightly with a coarse cloth to remove the salt-crystals. Then 
soak over night in tepid water. Exchange this in the morn- 
ing for ice-cold, and let the fish lie in the latter for three 
hours. Take it out, wipe dry, and cut in strips as directed 
in the foregoing receipt. Drop these, when all are ready, 
»Ki a saucepan of boiling water, placed alongside of a kettle 
eif pickle prepared as for fresh salmon. Beside these have 
your air-tight jars, covers laid in readiness, and when the 
salmon has boiled five minutes— fairly boiled, not simmered 
— fish out the pieces with your wire spoon, pack rapidly 
into your can; fill up with the boiling pickle from the 
other kettle, and seal instantly. In two days the pickled 
salmon wiU be fit for use, and is scarcely distinguishable 
from that made of fresh fish. It has the advantage of 
being always procurable, and of comparative cheapness, and 
in the country is a valuable stand-by in case of unexpected 
supper company.

Boiled Shad. {M-esh.) ^J* 

Clean, wash, and wipe the fish. A roe shad is best foi 
this purpose. Cleanse the roes tlioi-oughly, and having 
spiinkled both shad and eggs with salt, wrap in separate 
cloths and put into the fish-kettle, side by side.. Cover with 
salted water, and boil from half an hour to three-quarters, 
in proportion to the size. Experience is the best rule as to 
the time. Wlien you have once cooked fish to a turn, note 
the weight and time, and you will be at no loss thereafter. 
A good rule is to make a pencilled memorandum in the 
margin of the receipt-book opposite certain receipts. 

Serve the shad upon a hot dish, with a boat of drawn 
butter mingled with chopped egg and parsley, or egg-snuce. 
Lay the roes about the body of the fish. Gamiah witli 
tapers and slices of hard-boiled eggs.

Boiled Shad. {Salt.) 

In those States where shad are plenty they are salte^^ 
»nd used freely, as are salt mackerel elsewhere. They form 
» delightful breakfast-dish, and are welcome on the supper 
table on winter nights. 

Soak the fish six or seven hours in warm watei', 
BLanging it several times; wipe off all the salt and immerse 
in ice-cold water. When it has lain in this an hour, put 
into a fish-kettle with enough fresh water to cover it, and 
boil from fifteen to twenty minutes, in proportion to the 
si/e. Serve in a hot dish, with a large lump of butter 
spread over the fish. 

Broiled Shad. {Fresh.) »|« 

Wash, wipe, and split the fish. Sprinkle with salt and 
pepper, and lay it upon a buttered gridiron, inside down 
ward. When the lower side is browned, turn the fish. 
One of medium size will be done in about twenty minutes. 
Serve upon a hot dish, and lay a good piece of butter upon 

the fish. 

Broiled Shad. {Salt.) 

Soak over night in lukewarm water. Take out in the 
morning and transfer to ice-cold for half an hour. Wipe 
very dry, and broil as you do fresh shad.

Mayoitnaise. (Msh.) 

Take a pound or so of cold boiled fish (halibut, rock, ta

Baked Salmon-Trout. »J« 

Those who have eaten this prince of game fish in \Aie 
Adirondacks, within an hour after he has left the lake, will 
fcgree with me that he never has such justice done hini at 
any other time as when baked with cream. 

Handle the beauty with gentle respect while cleaning, 
washing, and wiping him, and lay him at full length, still 
respectfully, in a baking-pan, with just enough water to 
keep him from scorching. If large, score the back-bone 
with a sharp knife, taking care not to mar the comehness 
of his red-spotted sides. Bake slowly, basting often with 
butter and water. By the time he is done — and he should 
be so well-looked after that his royal robe hardly shows a 
B»Bm or rent, and the red spots are still distinctly visible- 
have ready in a saucepan a cup of cream — diluted with . $ 
few spoonfuls of hot water, lest it should clot in heating-

Fried Pickerel. »J« 

The pickerel ranks next to trout among game-fish, and 
should be fried in the same manner. Especially — and 1 
urge this with groaning of spirit, in remembrance of the

and sweetness utterly departed — especially, do not fry him 
slowly and too long ; and when he is done, take him out of 
the grease I

Fkied Cat-fish. »J« 

Skin, clean, and remove the heads. Sprinkle with salt,

Cat-fish cooked in this manner are sweet and savory —

Stewed Eels. »{« 

Inquire, before buying, where they were caught, and 
give so decided a preference to country eels as to refuse 
those fattened upon the offal of city wharves. Nor are the 
largest eels the best for eating. One weighing a pound is 
better for' your purpose than a bulky fellow that weighs 
three. 

Skin and clean, carefully extracting all the fat from the 
inside. Cut into lengths of an inch and a half; put into a 
saucepan, with enough cold water to cover them ; throw in 
a little salt and chopped parsley, and stew slowly, closely cov- 
ered, for at least one hour. Add, at the last, a great spoon- 
ful of butter, and a little flour wet with cold water, also pep- 
per. Serve in a deep dish. The apjsearance and odor of 
this stew are so pleasing as often to overcome the preju 
dices of those who " Wouldn't touch an eel for the world ! 
They look so lik<» snakes ! " And those who have tasted 
once rarely enter a second demun er. 

Fried Eels.

lard or dripping, to a light brown.

To Boil a Lobster. 

Choose a lively one — ^not too large, lest he stcaid l 
kingh. Put a handful of salt into a pot of boiling watei , 
and having tied the claws together, if your fish-m^rchani 
has not already skewered them, plimge him into the pre 
pai-ed bath. He will be restive under this vigorous hydro- 
pathic treatment ; but allay your tortured sympathies by 
the reflection that he is a cold-blooded animal, destitute of 
imagination, and that pain, according to some philosophers, 
exists only in the imagination. However this may be, his 
suflFering will be short-lived. BoU from half an hour to an 
hour, as his size demands. When done, draw oiit the scai-lei 
Innocent, and lay him, face downward, in a sieve to dry 
When cold, split open the body and tail, and crack the clawn 
to extract the meat, throwing away the " lady-fingers " au • 
the head. Lobsters are seldom served without dressing, uj-- 
on private tables, as few persons care to take the trouble o* 
preparing their own salad after taking their seats at th« 
board. 

Devilled Lobster.

mustard, cayenne, salt, and some pungent sauce. -Toss and 
stir until it is well mixed, and put into a porcelain sauce- 
pan (covered), with just enough hot water to keep it from 
burning. Rub the coral smooth, moistening with vinegai 
until it is thin enough to pour easily, then stir into the con 
touts of the saucepan. It is necessary to prepare the dress 
ing, let me say, before the lobster-meat is set on the fira 
£t ought to boil up but once before the coral and vinegai 
*re put in. Next stir in a heaping tablespoonful of butter

"Water-Turtles, or Terrapins. 

Land-terrapins, it is hardly necessary to say, are uneat- 
able, but the large turtle that frequents our mill-ponds and 
divers can be converted into a relishable article of food. 

Plunge the turtle into a pot of boiling water, and lei 
him lie there five minutes. You can then skin the under- 
part easily, and pull off the horny parts of the feet. Laj 
bim for ten minutes in cold salt and water ; then put into 
more hot water — salted, but not too much. Boil until 
tender. The time will depend upon the size and age. 
Take him out, drain, and wipe dry ; loosen the shell carefully, 
not to break the flesh ; cut open also with care, lest yoa 
touch the gall-bag with the knife. Remove this with the 
entrails and sand-bag. Cut up all the rest of the animal 
into small bits, season with pepper, salt, a chopped onion, 
sweet herbs, and a teaspoonful of some spiced sauce, or a 
tablespoonful of catsup — walnut or mushroom. Save the 
juice that runs from the meat, and put all together into 
» saucepan with a closely-fitting top. Stew gently fifteeu 
ijninutes, stirring occasionally, and add a great spoonful of 
butter, or a teaspoonful browned flour wet in cold water, a 
glass of brown sherry, and lastly, the beaten yolk of an 
egg, mixed with a little of the hot liquor, that it mav not

vnrdle. BoU up once, and turn into a covered dish. Send 
around green pickles and delicate slices of dry toast with it. 

Drain the liquor from two quarts of firm, plump oysters; 
liiii with it a small teacupful of hot water, add a little salt 
and pepper, and set over the fire in a sancepan. When it 
comes to a boil, add a large cupful of rich milk. (Cream is

melted and well stirred in, take the saucepan from the fire. 
>Serve with oyster or cream crackers, as soon as possible. 
Oysters become tough and tasteless when cooked too much, 
or left to stand too long after they are withdrawn from the 
fire. A good and safe plan is, to heat the milk in a sepa- 
rate vessel set in another of hot water, and after it is 
mingled with the liquor and oysters, stir assiduously or it 
may " catch," as the cooks say — i. e., scorch on the sides cr 
bottom of the saucepan.

Oystee Fritters. »}«

Oyster C'melet. »!• 

12 oysters, if large ; double the number of siia.1 ones. 

6 (iggs. 

1 cup milk. 

1 tablespoonful butter. 

Chopped parsley, salt, and pepper. 

Chop the oysters very finely. Beat the yolks and whiteg 
of the eggs separately as for nice cake — the white until it 
Btands in a heap. Put three tablespoonfuls of butter in a 
frying-pan, and heat while you are mixing the omelet. Stir 
the milk into a deep dish with the yolk, and season. Next 
|,'it in the chopped oysters, beating vigorously as you add 
them gradually. When they are thoroughly incorporated, 
pour in the spoonful of melted butter ; finally, whip in the 
whites lightly and with as few strokes as possible. If the 
oiitter is hot, and it ought to be, that the omelet may not 
Btaud uncooked, put the mixture into the pan. Do not stir 
it, but when it begins to stiffen — " to set," in culinaiy 
phrase, slip a broad-bladed, round-pointed dinner-knife 
around the sides, and cautiously under the omelet, that the 
butter may reach every part. As soon as the centre ia 
fairly " set," turn out into a hot dish. Lay the latter bot- 
tom upward over the frying-pan, which must be turned up 
Bide-down dexterously. This brings the browned side a' 
the omelet uppermost. This omelet is delicious and easily 
made.

V Pickled Oysters.\»J« 

100 large oysters. 

1 pint white wine vinegar. 

1 doz. blades of mace. 

2 doz. whole cloves. 

2 doz. whole black peppers. 

1 large red pepper broken into bits. 

Put oysters, liquor and all, into a porcelain- or bell-metal 
kettle. Salt to taste. Heat slowly until the oysters are 
very hot, but not to boiling. Take them out with a perfo- 
rated skimmer, and set aside to cool. To the liquor which 
remains in the kettle add the vinegar and spices. Boil up 
fairly, and when the oysters are almost cold, pour over them 
scalding hot. Cover the jar in which they are, and put 
away in a cool place. Next day put the pickled oysteis into 
glass cans with tight tops. Keep in the dark, and wnere 
tliey are not liable to become heated. 

I, have kept oysters thus prepared for three weeks in th« 



78 CX3MM0N SENSE. 

winter. If yon open a can, use the contents up as s(.on 
BS practicable. The air, like the light, will turn thesio 
dark. 

It is little trouble for every housekeeper to put up the 
pickled oysters needed ia her family ; and besides the satis- 
faction she will feel in the consciousness that the materials 
used are harmless, and the oysters sound, she will save a* 
least one-third of the price of those she would buy ready 
pickled. The coloi-less vinegar used by " professionals " fot 
such purposes is usually sulphuric or pyroHgneous acid. li 
you doubt this, pour a little of the liquor from the pickled 
oysters put up by your obliging oyster-dealer into a bell- 
metal kettle. I tried it once, and the result was a liquid 
that matched the clear green of Niagara in hue. I cannot 
compare it justly with anything else. 

Roast Oysters. 

There is no pleasanter frolic for an Autumn evening, in 
the regions where oysters are plentiful, than an impiomptu 
" roast " in the kitchen. There the oysters are hastily 
thrown into the fire by the peck. You may consider that 
your fastidious taste is marvellously respected if they are 
washed first. A bushel basket is set to receive the empty 
shells, and the click of the oyster-knives forms a constant 
accompaniment to the music of laughing voices. Nor are 
roast oysters amiss upon your own quiet supper-table, when 
the " good man " comes in on a wet night, tired and hungry, 
and wants " something heartening.'' Wash and wipe the 
Bhell-oysters, and lay them in the oven, if it is quick ; upou 
the top of the stove, if it is not. When they open, thoy 
are done. Pile in a large dish and send to table. Remove 
the upper shell by a dextero\is wrench of the knife, season 
the oyster on the lower, with popper-sauce and butter, or 
poj.'jor, salt, and vinegar in lieu of the sauce, and you ha^t

POULTRY, 



Poultry should never be eaten in less than six ov eight 
hours after it is killed ; but it should be picked and drawn 
as soon as possible. Tliere is no dii-er disgrace to our 
Noi'them markets than the practice of sending whole dead 
fowls to market. I have bought such from responsible 
poultry-dealers, and found them uneatable, from having re- 
maini-d undrawn until the flavor of the craw aird intestinei 
had impregnated the whole body. Those who are conver 
sant with the habit of careful country housewi%es, of keep 



PO0LTBT. 83 

ing ap a fowl withoac food for a day and niglit before kill 
ing aaJ dressing for their own eating, cannot but regard 
with disgust the surcharged crops and puffy sides of thosf 
Bold hy weight in the shambles. If you want to know what 
you really pay for poultry bought in these circumstances, 
weigh the offal extracted from the fowl by your cook, and 
dndiict from the market weight. " But don't you know it 
actually poisons a fowl to lie so long undressed ? " once ex- 
claimed a Southern lady to me. " In our markets they are 
offeied for sale ready picked and drawn, with the giblets — 
also cleaned — tucked under their wings." 

1 know nothing about the poisonous nature of the en- 
•rails and crops. I do asyjrt that the custom is unclean 
•lad unjust. And this I do without the remotest hope of 
■I'ousing my fellow-housekeepers to remonstrance against 
irttablished usage. Only it relieves my mind somewhat to 
RTumble at what I cannot help. The best remedy I can 
propose for the grievance is to buy Kve fowls, and, before 
Bonding them home, ask your butcher to decapitate them ; 
siie probabilities being greatly in favor of the supposition 
that your cook is too "tinder-hearted" to attempt the job. 

One word as to the manner of roasting meats and fowls. 
In tliis day of ranges and cooking-stoves, I think I am 
speaking within bounds when I assume that not one house- 
keeper in fifty uses a spit, or even a tin kitchen, for such 
[imposes. It is in vain that the writers of receipt-books 
Inform us with refreshing naivete that all our meats are 
baked, not roasted, and expatiate upon the siiperior flavor 
of those pre])ared upon the EngJisli spits and in old-fa*;h- 
ionod kitchens, where enormous wood-fires blazed from 
morning until night. I shall not soon forget my perplex- 
ity when, an inexperienced housekeeper and a firm beUevei 
In all " that was wiit " by older and wiser people, I stood 
before my neat Mott's " Defiance," a. fine siiloin of beef 



64 COMMON 8SNSB. 

ready to be cooked on the table behind me, and read front 
my Instruction-book that my " fire should extend at least 
eight inches beyond the roaster on either side ! " I am not 
denying the virtues of spits and tin kitchens — only regret- 
ting that they are not within the reach of every one. It 
view of this fact, let me remai-k, for the benefit of tho un- 
fortunate many, that, in the opinion of excellent judges, the 
practice of roastiRg meat in close ovens has advantages. 
Of these I need mention but two, to wit, the preservation 
of the flavor of the ai-ticle roasted, and the prevention of 
its escape to the ujjper regions of the dwelling. 

The directions hereafter given touching roasting will 
apply either to turnspit, " kitchens," or ovens. 

KoAST Turkey. 

After drawing the turkey, rinse out with several waters, 
and in next to the last mix a teaspoonful of soda. The in 
side of a fowl, especially if puichased in the market, is 
Bouietimes very sour, and imparts an unpleasant taste to the 
stuffing, if not to the inner part of the legs and sido bones. 
The soda will act as a coirective, and is moreover very 
cleansing. Fill the body with this water, shake well, empty 
it out, and rinse with fair water. Then prepare f- dressing 
of bread-crumbs, mixed with butter, pepper, salt, thyme or 
Bweet marjoram, and wet with hot water or milk. You 
may, if you like, add the beaten yolks of two eggs. A little 
chopped sausage is esteemed an improvement when well in- 
corporated ^^^.th the other ingredients. Or, mince a dozen 
oystei-s and stir into the dressing ; and, if you are partial to 
the taste, wet the bread-crumbs with the oyster-Hquor. 
The effect upon the turkey-meat, particularly that of the 
Ijieast, is very pleasant. 

Stufl' the craw with this, and tie a string tightly aboul 
the neck, to prevent the esoaj>e of the stuffing. Then fiU

thu body of the tmkey, and sew it up with strong tkiead 
This aud the neck-string are to be removed when the fowi 
is dished. In roasting, if your fire is brisk, allow about ten 
minutes to a pound ; but it will depend very much upon 
the turkey's age whether this rule holds good. Dredge it 
with flour before roasting, and baste often ; at first with 
butter and water, afterward with the gravy in the dripping- 
pan. If you roast in an oven, and lay the turkey in tho 
pan, put in with it a teacup of hot water. Many roast al- 
ways upon a grating placed on the top of the pan. In that 
case the boiling water steams the underj)art of the fowl, 
and prevents the skin from drying too fast, or cracking. 
Koast to a fine bro^vn, and if it threaten to darken too 
rapidly, lay a sheet of white paper over it until the lower 
part is also done. 

Stew the chopped giblets in just enough water to cover 
them, and when the turkey is lifted from the pan, add 
these, with the water in which they were boiled, to the 
drippings ; thicken with a spoonful of browned flour, wei 
with cold water to prevent lumping, boil up once, and pour 
into the gra\'y-boat. If the turkey is very fat, skim the

Boiled Chickens. 

Clean, wash, and stufi' as for roasting. Baste a floured 
cloth around each, and put into a pot with enough boiling 
water to cover them well. The hot water cooks the skin at 
once, and prevents the escape of the juices. The broti- 
will not be so rich as if the fowls are put on in cold water 
but this is a proof that the meat will be more nutrition ■ 
and better flavored. Stew very slowly, for the first haU 
hour especially. . Boil an hour or more, guiding yoursell 
by size and toughness.

Fkicasseed Chicken. {Brown.') •J^

Broiled Chicken. 

It is possible to render a tough fowl eatable by Doiling 
ct stewing it with care. Never broil such ! An 1 evei. 
(vhen assured that your " broiler " is young, it is wise t-c 
make this doubly svire by laying it upon sticks extending 
from side to side of a dripping-pan fvUl of boiling water. 
Set this in the oven, invert a tin pan over the chicken, and 
let it steam for half an hour. Tliis process relaxes the 
muscles, and renders supple the joints, besides preserving 
the juices that would be lost in parboiling. The chicken 
'ihould be split down the back, and wijjed perfectly dry 
"oefore it is steamed. Transfer from the vapor-bath to a 
Duttered gridiron, inside downward. Cover with a tin pan 
lor common plate, and broil until tender and brown, turning 
(leveral times ; from half to three-quartei-s of an hour will 
be sufficient. Put into a hot chafing-dish, and butter very 
weU. Send to table smoking hot.

Chicken and Ham. *{•»

To Use up Cold Duck. »{< 

I may say, as preface, that cold duck is in itself an ex

ofl en preferred to hot. If the duck has been cut into at all, 
di-\ide neatly into joints, and slice the breast, laying slices 
of dressing about it. Garnish with lettuce or parsley, and 
eat with jeUy. 

But if a warm dish is desired, cut the meat from the 
bones and lay it in a saucepan, with a little mmced cold 
ham ; pour on just enough water to cover it, and stir in a 
tablespoonful of butter. Cover, and heat gradually, until 
it is near boiling. Tlien add the gravy, diluted with a little 
hot water ; a gi-eat spoonful of catsup, one of Worcester- 
shire sauce, and one of currant or cranberry jelly, with a 
glass of wine alid a tablespoonful of browned flour. 

Or,

Guinea Fowls. 

Many are not aware what an excellent article of food 
these speckled Arabs of the poultry-yard are. They are 
kept chiefly for the beauty of theii- plumage, and their deli- 
cious eggs, which are far richer than those of chickens. 

Unless young they are apt to be tough, and the dark 
color of the meat is objected to by those who are not fond 
of, or used to eating game. Cooked according to the fore 
goiug receipt they are very savory, no matter how old thev 
may be. Put them on early, and stew slowly, and goou 
management will bring the desired end to pass. There ia 
nothing in the shape of game or poultry that is not ame- 
nable to this process, providing the salt be omitted until th* 
meat is tender. 

But a pair of young Guinea fowls, stufied and roasted, 
basting them with butter until they are half done, deserv 
an honorable place upon our bill of fare. Season the gravy 
with a chopped shallot, parsley, or summer savory, not 
omitting the ininced giblets, and thicken with browned 
flour. Send around currant, or other tart jelly, with the 
fowL A little ham, minced iine, improves the dressing.

Goose Pie. 

An old goose is as nearly good for nothing as it is possi- 
ble for anything which was once valuable, and is not now 
absolutely spoiled, to be. The best use to put it to is tc 
make it into a j)ie, in the following manner. Put on the 
ancient early in the morning, in cold water enough to cover 
it, unsalted, having cut it to pieces at every joint. Warm it 
up gradually, and let it stew — not boil hard — for four or five 
hours. Should the water need replenishing, let it be done 
from the boiling kettle. Parboil a beef 's tongue (smoked), 
cut into slices nearly half an inch thick ; also slice six hard- 
boiled eggs. Line a deep pudding-dish with a good paste ; 
•ay in the pieces of goose, the giblets chopped, the sliced 
tongue and egg, in consecutive layers ; season with pepper, 
salt, and bits of bvitter, and proceed in this order until the 
dish is fuU. If the goose is large, cut the meat from the 
bones after stewing, and leave out the latter entirelj'. In- 
terspea-se with strips of paste, and fill up with the gravy in 
which the goose was stewed, thickened with floui. Covei 
with a thick paste, and when it is done, brush over the top 
Siith beaten white of egg. 

In cold weather this pie wiU keep a week, and is verf 
good.

Stewed Pigeons. 

Pick, draw, clean and stuff as above directed. Put the 
pigeons in a deep pot with enough cold water to cover them, 
and stew gently for an hour, or until, testing them with a 
ifark, you find them tender. Then season with pepper, salt, 
lA few blades of mace, a Kttle sweet maijoram, and a good 
(jiece of butter. Stew, or rather simmer, for five minutes 
*rtnger — then stir in a tablespoonfid of browned flour. Let 
It boil up once ; remove the pigeons, draw out the strings 
with which they were sewed up, and serve, pouring the hot 
gravy over them. A little salt j)ork or ham, cut into strips, 
is an impi'ovement. This should be put in when the pigeons 
have stewed half an hour. 

Broiled Pigeons ob Squabs. 

Young pigeons or " squabs " are rightly esteemed a great 
delicacy. They are cleaned, washed, and dried carefidly with 
a clean cloth ; then split down the back, and broiled like 
chickens. Season with pepper and salt, and butter liberally 
in dishing them. They are in great request in a convalesi 
cent's room, being peculiarly savory and nourishing. 

They may, for a change, be roasted whole, according to 
the receipt for roast pigeons.

EoAST Beef. 



The best pieces for roasting are the sii-loin and rib piece*. 
The latter are oftenest used by small families. Make jom 
butcher remove most of the hone, and skewer the meat into 
the shape of a round. If you roast in an oven, it is a good 
plan to dash a small cup of hoUing water over the meat in 
first putting it down, letting it trickle into the pan. This, 
for a season, checks the escape of the juices, and aUows the 
meat to get warmed through befoi-e the top dries by said 
escape. If there is much fat upon the upper surface, cover 
with a paste of flour and water imtil it is. nearly done. 
Baste frequently, at first with salt and water, afterward with 
the drippings. Allow about a quarter of an hour to a 
pound, if you like your meat rare ; more, if you prefer to 
have it well done. Some, when the meat is almost done, 
dredge with flour and baste with butter — only once. 

Remove the beef, when quite ready, to a heated dish ; 
skim the drippings ; add a teacupful of boUing water, boil 
up once, and send to table in a gravy-boat. Many reject 
made gravy altogether, and only serve the red Jdquor thai 
runs from the meat into the dish as it is cut. This is th.a 
practice with some — indeed most of our best housekeepei-s. 
If you have made gravy in a sauce-boat, give your guest hi* 
(iioice between that and the juice in the dish. 

Serve with mustard, or scraped horse- radish and vinegar

Beef-Steak. 

It is not customary to fry beef-steaks for people ■whc 
know what really good cookery is. To speak niore plainly, 
a steak, Mlled by heat and swimming in grease, is a culinary 
solecism, both vulgar and indigestible. 

Cut the steak thick, at least three-quarters of an inch in 
thickness, and if you cannot get tender meat for this pur- 
pose, it is best to substitute some other dish for it. Eat 
liuce tender meat is not ^.Iways to be had, if the piece you 
have purchased is doubtful, lay it on a clean cloth, take a 
blunt heavy carving-knife, if you have not a steak malldt, 



100 COJIHON BEN8E. 

Bnd back closely from one end to the other ; then turn and 
repeat the process upon the other side. The knife should 
be so dull you cannot cut with it, and the strokes not the 
sixtieth part of an inch apart. Wipe, but not wash, and 
ky on a buttered gridiron over a clear fire, turning ver^ 
often as it begins to drip. Do not season until it is done, 
wliich will be in about twelve minutes, if the fire is good 
and the cook attentive. Rub your hot chafing-dish with a 
split raw onion, lay in the steak, salt and pepper on both 
sides, and put a liberal lump of butter upon the upper, 
Then put on a hot cover, and let it stand five minutes to 
draw the juices to the svirface before it is eaten. If you 
have neither chafing-dish nor cover, lay the steak between 
two hot platters for the same time, sending to table without 
uncovering. A gridiron fitting under the grate is better 
than any other. If a gridiron is not at hand, rub a little 
butter upon the bottom of a hot, clean frying-pan, put in 
the meat, set over a bright fire, and turn frequently. This 
will not be equal to steak cooked upon a gridiron, but it is 
Infinitely preferable to the same fried. 

I shall never forget the wondering distrust with which 
my first cook, a sable " professional," watched me when 1 
undertook to show her how to prepare a steak for the third 
breakfast over which I presided as mistress of ceremonies. 
And when, at the end of twelve minutes, I removed the 
meat, " rare and hot," to the heated dish in readiness, her 
Bnifi" of lofty contempt was eloquent as indescribable. 

" Call dat cooked ! Folks 'bout here would 'a had dat 
steak on by day-break !" 

A remark that has been recalled to my mind hundreds 
of times since at the tables of so-called capital housewives. 

riie best — nay, the only pieces for steak are those known 
AS porter-house and sirloin. The former is the more highly 
esteemed by gourmands ; but a really tender sirloin is mort 



BBBF. 101 

Berviceable where there axe several persons in .he family, 
the porter-house having a narrow strip of extremely nic€ 
meat lying next the bone, while the rest is often inferior t* 
any part of the sirloin. 

Beef-steak and Onions. 

Prepare the steak as above directed. While it is broiling 
put three or four chopped onions in a frying-pan with a lit- 
tle beef-dripping or butter. Stir and shake them briskly 
until they are done, and begin to brown. Dish your steak 
and lay the onions thickly on top. Cover and let all stand 
five or six minutes, that the hot onions may impart the re- 
quired flavor to the hot meat. In helping your guests, in- 
quire if they will take onions with the slices of steak put 
upon their plates. I need hardly remind the sensible cook 
how necessary it is to withdraw the gridiron from the fire 
for an iustant, should the fat drip upon the coals below, and 
smoke or blaze. Yet those who have eaten steaks flavored 
with creosote may thank me for the suggestion. 

Beef A-i^a-mode. *J« 

Take a round of beef; remove the bone from the middle, 
and trim away the tougher bits about the edges, with such 
gristle, (fee., as you can reach. Set these aside for soup- 
stock. 

Bind the beef into a symmetrical shape by passing a 
strip of stout muslin, as wide as the round is high, about it, 
and stitching the ends together at one side. Have ready at 
least a pound of fat salt pork, cut into strips as thick aa 
your middle finger, and long enough to reach from top to 
bottom of *ne trussed round. Put a half pint of vinegar 
over the fire in a tin or porcelain saucepan ; season with 
tliree or four minced shallots or button onions, two tear 
snoonfils made mustard, a teaspoonful nutmeg, one ol 



102 COMMON SENSE. 

do-ses, half as much allspice, half- spoonful bkck pepper') 
with a bunch of s'weet herbs minced fine, and a tablespoon- 
fill brown sugar. Let all simmer for five minutes, then boil 
np once, and pour, while scalding hot, upon the strips of 
pork, which should be laid in a deep dish. Let all stand to 
gother until cold. Remove the pork to a plate, and mix with 
Hie liquor left in the dish enough bread-crumbs to makt 
a tolerably stiff force-meat. If the vinegar is very strong, 
dilute with a little water before moistening the crumbs. 
With a long, thin-bladed knife, make perpendicular incisions 
in the meat, not more than half an inch apart, even nearer 
is better; thrvist into these the strips of fat pork, so far 
down that the upper ends are just level with the surface, 
and work into the cavities with them a little of the force- 
meat. Proceed thus until the meat is fairly riddled and 
plugged with the pork. Fill the hole from which the bone 
was taken with the dressing and bits of pork ; rub the up- 
per side of the beef well with the spiced force-meat. Put 
iato a baking-pan, with a little water to prevent burning ; 
turn a large pan over it to keep in the steam, and roast 
slowly for five or six hours, allowing half an hour to each 
pound of meat. If the beef be tough, you had better stew 
the round by putting it in a pot with half enough water to 
cover it. Cover tightly and stew very slowly for six hours ; 
then set in the oven with the gravy about it, and brown 
half an hour, basting frequently. 

. If you roast the round, do not remove the cover, except 
to baste (and this should be done often), until fifteen min- 
utes before you draw it from the oven. Set away with the 
muslin band still about it, and pour the gravy over the meat. 
When cold, lift from the gravy, — which, by the rra.j, 
will be excellent seasoning for your soup-stock, — cut the 
stitches in the muslin girdle, and remov carefully and 
Bcnd the meat to table, cold, garnished with parsley and

a shaving. Do not offer the outside to any one ; but th«

I cannot too highly commend this as a side-dish at din 
ner, and a supper and breakfast stand-by. In winter it 
will keep a week and more, and as long in summer, if kepi 
in the refrigerator — except when it is on the table. 

Breakfast Stew of Beef. ►{« 

Cut up two pounds of beef — ^not too lean — into pieces 
a,n inch long ; put them into a saucepan with just enough 
water to cover them, and stew gently for two hours. Set 
away until next morning, when season with pepper, salt, 
sweet maijoram or summer savory, chopped onion, and 
parsley. Stew half an hour longer, and add a teaspoon- 
ful of sauce or catsup, and a tablespoonful of browned 
flour wet up with cold water ; finally, if you wish to have 
it very good, half a glass of wine. Boil up once, and pour 
into a covered deep dish. ■ 

This is an economical dish, for it can be made of the 
liommoner parts of the beef, and exceedingly nice for winter 
breakfasts. Eaten with corn-bread and stewed potatoes, it 
?vill soon win its way to a place in the " stock company " 
of every judicious housewife

Beef Hash. 

To two parts cold roast or boiled corned beef, chopped 
fine, put one of mashod potatoes, a little pepper, salt, milk, 
and melted butter. Turn all into a fryiag-pan, and stir un- 
til it is heated through and smoking hot, but not until it 
browns. Put into a deep dish, and if stiff enough, shape 
as you would mashod potato, into a hillock. 

Or, you can cease stirring for a few minutes, and let 
a brown crust form on tlie under side; then turn out 
whole into a flat dish, the brown side uppermost. 

Or, mould the mixture into flat cakeS; dip these in 
beaten eggs and fry in hot drippings. 

The remains of beef it-la-mode are very good prepared 
in any of these ways. A little catsup and mustard are an 
improvement to plain cold beef, thus hashed. 

Beef-steak Pie. 

Cut the steak into pieces an inch long, and stew with 
the bone (cracked) in just enough water to cover the meat 
until it is half done. At the same time parboil a dozer, 
potatoes in another pot. If you wish a bottom crust — a 
doubtful question — line a pudding-dish with a good paste, 
made according to the receipt given below. Put in a layer 
of the beef, with salt and pepper, and a very little chopped 
onion; then one of sliced potatoes, with a little butter 
scattered upon them, and so on, until the dish is full 
Pour over all the gravy in which the meat is stewed, hav- 
ing first thrown away the bone and thickened with browned 
flour. Cover with a crust thicker than the lower, leaving 
« slit in the middle.

Crust for Meat-Pies. »J« 

1 quart of flour. 

3 tablespoonfuls of lard. 

2-J- cups milk. 

1 teaspoonful of soda wet with hot water, and 
stirred into the milk. 

2 teaspoonfuls of cream-tartar sifted into the dry 
flour. 

1 teaspoonful of salt. 

Work up very lightly and quickly, and do not get 
too stiff. 

Beef-Pie, with Potato Crust. ►{« 

Mince some rare roast beef or cold corned beef, if it ii 
not too salt ; season with pepper and salt, and spread a 
liyer in the bottom of a pudding-dish. Over this put one 
of mashed potato, and stick bits of butter thickly all over 
it ; then another of meat, and so on until you are ready 
for the crust. 

To a large cupful of mashed potato add two tablespoon- 
fuls of melted butter, a weU-beaten egg, two cups of nulk, 
and beat all together until very light. Then work in 
enough flour to enable you to roll out in a sheet — ^not toa 
stiff — and, when you have added to the meat and potato in 
the dish a gravy made of warm water, butter, milt, and 
catsup, with what cold gravy or dripping remains from the 
" roast," cover the pie with a thick, tender crust, cutting a 
Blit in the middle. 

You can use the potato crust, which is very wholesome 
and "ood, for any kind of meat-pie. It looks weU bruslied 
•vei with beaten white of egg before it goes to table.

Beef's Heakt. 



Wash the heart well, and cut into sqnaies half an iiich 
long. Stew them for ten minutes in enough water to cover 
fchem. Salt the water slightly to di-aw out the blood, and 
throw it away as it rises in scum to the top. Take out the 
meat, strain the liquor, and return the chopped heart to it, 
with a sliced onion, a great spoonful of catsup, some pars 
ley, a head of celery chopped fine, and cayenne pejjpei, 
with a large lump of butter. Stew until the meat is very 
tender, when add a tablespoonfiil of browned flour to 
thicken. Boil up once, .and serve. 

To Corn Beef. 

Riib each piece of beef weU with salt mixed with one- 
tenth part of saltpetre, until the salt lies dry upon the sur- 
fcice. Put aside ia a cold place for twenty-four hours, and 
repeat the process, rubbing in the mixture very thorouglily. 
Put away again \mtil the next day, by which time the 
pickle should be ready. 

5 gallons of water. 

1 gallon of salt. 

4 ounces saltpetre. 

1^ lb. brown sugar. 

Boil this briue ten minutes ; let it get perfectly cold ; then 
pour over the beef, having wiped the latter entirely dry. 

Examine the pickle from time to time to see if it keeps 
well ; if not, take out the meat without delay, wipe it, and 
rub in dry salt, covering it well until you can prepare new 
and stronger brine.

Beef Tongue, 

Soak over night in cold water when you have washed 
it well. Next morning put into a pot with plenty of cold 
water, and boil slowly until it is tender throughout. This 
you can determine by testing it with a fork. 

When it is cold, pare off the thick skin, cut in round 
slices, and dish for tea, garnishiag with fresh parsley. 

Tongue sandwiches are generally held in higher esteera 
than those made of ham. 

Dried Beef. 

The most common way of serving dried or smoked 
beef is to shave it into thin slices or chips, raw ; but a 
more savory relish may be made of it with little trouble. 

Put the slices of uncooked beef into a frying-pan with 
just enough boiling water to cover them ; set them over 
the fire for ten minutes, drain off all the water, and with s 
knife and fork cut the meat into small bits. Return to 
the pan, which should be hot, with a tablespoonful of but- 
ter «nd a little pepper. Have ready some well-beaten eggs, 
illowiag four to a half-pound of beef; stir them into the 
pan with the minced meat, and toss and stir the mixtura 
for about two minutes. Send to table in a covered dish.

MUTTON AJSB LAMB, 
Roast Motton. 

The parts which are usually roasted are :— < 

The shoulder, 

The saddle, or cMne, and 

The loin and hauuch (a leg and part of the loin). 

The leg is best boiled, unless the mutton is young and 
rery tender. Wash the meat well, and dry with a clean 
cloth. Let your fire be clear and strong ; put the meat on 
with a little water in the dripping-pan. If you think well 
of the plan (and I do), let this be a cupful of boiling water 
dashed over the meat when it is first put down to roast, 
and left to trickle into the pan. I have elsewhere explained 
the advantages of the method. Allow, in roastiilg, about 
twelve minutes per pound, if the fire is good. Baste often 
— at fii-st with salt and water, afterward with the gravy. 
If it is in danger of browning too fast, cover with a large 
sheet of white paper. Roast lamb in the same manner, 
but not so long. Skim the gravy well, and thicken very 
slightly with browned flour. Serve with currant jelly. 

Roast Muttov a la Venison. 

A Christmas saddle of mutton is very fine, prepared as 
follows : "Wash it well, inside and out, with vinegar. Do 
not wipe it, but hang it up to dry in a cool cellar. When 
the vinegar has dried off, throw a clean cloth over it, to 
keep out the dust. On Ihe next day but one, take down 
the meat and sponge it over again with vinegar, then put 
it back in its place in the cellar. Repeat this process thi^ec 
times a week for a fortnight, keeping the meat hung in a 
cold place, and covered, except while you are washing it, 
Whon you are ready to cook it, wipe it off with a drj 
doth, but do not wash it. Roast — ^basting for the firai 



MUTTON AKD LAMB. 109 

hour with butter and water; afterward with the gravy, 
and keeping the meat covered with a large tin pan for twc 
hours. A large saddle of mutton will require four hours to 
roast. When it is done, remove to a dish, and cover tc 
keep it hot. Skim the gravy, and add half a teacupful 
of waJnut, mushroom, or tomato catsup, a glass of Ma- 
deira wine, and a tahlespoonful of browned flour. Boil 
up once, and send to table in a sauce-boat. Always send 
around currant or some other tart jelly with roast mutton. 
If properly cooked, a saddle of mutton, prepared in accord- 
ance with these directions, will strongly resemble venison 
in taste. An old Virginia gentleman whom I used to 
know, always hung up the finest saddle his plantation 
could furnish six weeks before Christmas, and had it 
sponged off with vinegar every other day, untU the morn- 
ing of the important 25th ; and the excellence of his mut- 
ton was the talk of the neighborhood. It can certaiidy be 
kept a fortnight anywhere at that season. 

Boiled Mutton. 

"Wash a leg of mutton clean, and wipe dry. Do not 
leave the knuckle and shank so long as to be unshapely. 
Put into a pot with hot water (salted) enough to cover it, 
and boil until you ascertain, by probing with a fork, that 
it is tender in the thickest part. Skim off all the scum aa 
it rises. Allow about twelve minutes to each pound. Take 
from the fire, drain perfectly dry, and serve with melted 
butter, with capers, or nasturtium seed ; or, if you have 
neither of these, some cucumber or gherkin-pickle ttirred 
Into it. If you wish to use the broth for soup, put in very 
little salt while boiling ; if not, salt well, and boil th« meat 
in a cloth.

Mutton Ste^. »{« 

Cut up from tbiee to four pounds of mutton, — the in- 
ferior portions will do as well as any other, — crack th« 
bones, and remove aU the fat. Put on the meat — the 
pieces not more than an inch and a half in length — ^in a poi 
with enough cold water to cover well, and set it where it 
will heat gradually. Add nothiTig else until it has ste^^ed 
an hour, closely covered ; then throw in half a pound of 
salt pork cut into strips, a chopped onion, and some pep- 
per ; cover and stew an hour longer, or until .ne meat ia 
very tender. Make out a little paste, as for the crust of 
a meat-pie ; cut into squares, and drop in the stew. Boii 
ten minutes, and season further by the addition of a little 
parsley and thyme. Thicken with two spoonfuls of flour 
stirred into a cup of cold milk. Boil up once, and serve 
in a tureen or deep covered dish. 

If green com is in season, this stew is greatly improved 
by adding, an hour before it is taken from the fire, the 
grains of half a dozen ears, cut from the cob. 

Try it for a cheap family dinner, and you will repeat 
the experiment often. Lamb is even better for your pur- 
pose than mutton. 

Mutton Chops. 

If your butcher has not done it, — ^and the chances aro 
that he has not, unless you stood by to see it attended to, — 
trim off the superfluous fat and skin, so as to give the 
chops a certain Ktheness and elegance of sbape. Dip each 
in beaten egg, roll in pounded cracker, and fry in hot lard 
or dripping. If the fat is unsalted, sprinkle the chops 
with salt before rolling in the egg. Serve up dry and hot. 

Or, 

You may omit the egg and cracker, and broil on a gridiron 
over a bright fire. Put a Kttle salt and pej-per upon each

Mutton Cutlets, {^aked.) 

Cut them from the neck, and trim neatly. Lay aside 
the bits of bone and meat you cut off, to msike gravy. 
Pour a little melted butter over the cutlets, and let thom 
lie in it for fifteen minutes, keeping them just warm enough 
to prevent the butter from hardening ; then dip each in

dripping-pan with a very little water at the bottom. Bake 
quickly, and baste often with butter and water. Put on 
ite bones, &c., in enough cold water to cover them; stew, 
and season with sweet herbs, pepjier, and salt, with a spoon- 
ful of tomato catsup. Strain when all the substance ia 
extracted from the meat and bones ; thicken with browned 
ftour, and pour over the cutlets when they are served. 

( Mutton Ham. i 

For a leg of mutton weighing 12 lbs., take — 

1 ounce of black pepper, or -J- ounce of cayenne, 
J lb. brown sugar, 
1 ounce saltpetre, 
li lb. salt. 

The day after the sheep is killed, mix the sugar, pepper, 
and saltpetre, and rub weU into the meat for nearly fifteen 
minutes, until the outer part of it is thoroughly impreg 
Dated with the seasoning. Put the ham into a large earth- 
enware vessel, and cover it with the. salt. Let it remain 
thus for three weeks, turning it every day and basting it 
with the brine ; adding to this, after the first week, a tear^ 
supful of vinegar. When . the ham is removed from th»
