SUNDRIES. 
Cleaning Pots, Kettles, and Tins. 

Boil a double handful of hay or grass in a new iron pot; 
before attempting to cook with it ; scrub out with soap and 
sand ; then set on full of fair water, and let it boil half an 
nour. After this, you may use it without fear. As soon 
»s you empty a pot or frying-pan of that which has been 
^oked in it, fill with hot or cold water (hot is best) and 
aet back upon the fire to scald tlioroughly. 

New tins should stand near the fire with boiling water 
in them, in wldch has been dissolved a spoonful of soda, 
for an hour ; then be scoured inside with soft soap ; after- 
ward riused with hot water. Keep them clean by rubbing 
with sifted wood-ashes, or whitening. 

Oopper utensils should be cleaned with brickdust and 
flannel. 

Never set a vessel in the pot-closet without cleanicg and 
wiping it thoroughly. If grease be left in it, it will grow 
roncid. If set aside wet, it is apt to rust. 

Knives. 

Clean with, a soft flannel and Bath brick. If rusty, use 
•rwd-ashiis, rubbed on with a newly cut bit of Irish potato 
Tiiis w^ill i-emove spots when nothinsj else will. Keep yonr 



BUNORIES. 529 

best set wrapped in soft wkite paper ; then in linen, in : 
drawer out of damp and dust. 

Never dip the ivory handles of knives in hot water, 

Silver, 

Wash, after each meal, all that is soiled, in very hot soft 
water, with hard soap. "Wipe hard and quickly on a clean 
towel; then polish with dry flannel. If discolored with 
egg, mustard, spinach, or beans, or by any other means, 
rub out the stain with a stiff toothbrush (used only for this 
purpose), and silver soap. 

For years I have used no other preparation for cleaning 
silver than the Indexical silver soap, applied as I have 
described. After rubbing with a stiff lather made with 
this, wash off with hot water, wipe and polish while hot. 
There is no need for the weekly silver cleaning to be an 
event or a bugbear, if a little care and watchfulness be 
observed after each meal. Silver should never be allowed 
to grow dingy. If Bridget or Chloe will not attend prop- 
erly to this matter, take it in hand yourself. Have your 
own soap-cups — ^two of them — one with common soap, the 
other with a cake of silver soap in the bottom. Have for 
one a mop, for the other a stiff brush — a toothbrush is best. 
Use your softest towels for silver. 

Besides being clean and easy of application, the silver 
soap will not wear away the metal as will whiting or chalk, 
or plate-powder, however finely pulverized. 

China aijd Glass. 

There are few of the minor crooks in the lot of tha 
eareful housewife that cause her more anxiety and more 
discouragement than the attempt to teach domestics how tc 
wadi up dishes. 
23 



680 COMMON SENSE. 

"I've heard that Mrs. is very exact about soma 

things, such as washing up dishes and the likes of tha* 1 '' 
said a woman to me, with an affected laugh, having called 
to apply for the then vacant position of cook in my kitchen. 
She had high recommendations, a whine engrafced upon 
ner native brogue, and spoke of me in the third person— « 
trick of cheap (and bogus) gentiHty that tries my nerves 
and temper to the very marrow of my spine. "I was 

a-saying to myself, as I came along, that Mrs. must 

have been very onlucky in her girls if she had to '■ache 
tliem how to wash up dishes. I always thought that was 
one of the things that came kinder natural to every cook." 

" Mrs. 's" experience goes to prove that the wrong 

way of doing this must "come natural" to the class men- 
tioned, and that Nature is mighty in woman. The fact 
that the right way is noi to pile unrinsed dishes and 
plates in a big pan with a loose bit of soap on top, and 
pour lukewarm water over all ; then with a bit of rag to 
splash said water over each separately, and make another 
pile of them upon the kitchen-table, until the last is drawn, 
reeking with liqiiid grease, sticky and streaming, from the 
now filthy puddle of diluted swiU ; then to rub them lightly 
and leisurely with one towel — be they many or few — is a* 
difficult of comprehension to the scullionly mind as would 
be a familiar lecture upon the pons asinorum. 

Yet the right and only neat method is so simple and 
easy ! RLase the greasy plates, and whatever is sticky with 
sugar or other sweet, in hot water and transfer to a larger 
pan of very hot. Wash glass first ; next silver; then china 
— one article at a time, although you may put several in 
the pan. Have a mop with a handle ; rub upon the soap 
(over which the water should have been poured) until you 
have strong suds ; wash bo-'ih sides of plate and saucei , and 
wipe before putting it out of yoii/r hand. Draining leaves 



BUITOBIEB. 631 

Btieaka which can be felt by sonsitive finger-tips, if tot 
seen. If china is rough to the touch, it is dirty. Hot, 
clean suds, a dry, clean towel, and quick wiping leave it 
bright and shining. Roll your glasses around in the water, 
filling them as soon as they touch it, and you need never 
crack one. A lady did once explain the dinginess of hei 
goblets to me by saying that she was " afraid to put them 
in hot water. It rots glass and makes it so tender ! I 
prefer to have them a little cloudy." This is literally truf 
— that she said it, I mean. Certainly not that a year's soak 
in hot water could make glass tender. 

Washing Windows. 

Dissolve a little washing-soda in the water if thw glasa 
is very dim with smoke or dirt. Do not let it run on the 
sash, but wash each pane with old flannel; dry quickly 
with a soft, clean towel, wiping the corners with especial 
care. Polish with chamois skin, or newspapers rubbed «)ft 
between the hands. 

To Cleau Carpets. 

Sprinkle the carpet with tea leaves ; sweep well ; tboM 
use soap and soft, warm water for the grease and dirt spott 
This freshens up old carpets marvellously. Rub the wa'-f 
spots diy with a clean cloth. 

To Clean Paint. 

Scour with a flat brush, less harsh than that nsed for 
floors, using warm soft suds; before it dries wash off with 
old Bannel dipped in clean cold water, and wipe dry with a 
linen towel or cloth. Go thi-ough the whole process quickly, 
Gxti, the water may not dry upon and streak the paint. 



S33 OOMMOM SEWSK. 



To Keep Wooi.Eifs. 



Beat out all the dust, and Run for a day; shake ^erJ 
hard ; foil neatly and pin — or, what is better, sew up — closely 
in muslia or linen cloths, putting a small lump of gum 
eamphor in the centre of each bundle. Wrap newspapers 
about all, pinning so as to exclude dust and insects. 

These are really all the precautions necessary for the 
gafety even of furs, if they are strictly obeyed. But you 
may set moths at defiance if you can, in addition to these, 
secure, as a packing-case, a whiskey or alcohol barrel but 
lately emptied, and still strongly scented by the liquor. 
Have a close head, and fit it in neatly. Set away in the 
garret, and think no more of your treasures until next 
winter. 

To Wash Doubtful Calicoes. 

Put a teaspoonful of sugar of lead into a pailful of 
water, and soak fifteen minutes before washing. 

To Clean a Cloth Coat. 

Kub soap upon the wristbands and collar ; dip them in 
boiling-hot suds — and scrub mth a stiff clean brush. 
Treat the grease and dirt spots in the same way. Change 
the suds for clean and hot as it gets dirty. Wet and brnsh 
the whole coat, the right way of the cloth, with fresh suds, 
when you have scoured out the spots, adding three or four 
tablespoonfuls of alcohol to tlie water. Stretch the sleeves, 
pocket-holes, wristbands, and collar into shape, folding the 
deeves as if they had been ironed, also the collar. Lay 
apon a clean cloth, spread upon tlie table or floor, and let 
it get perfectly dry in the shade, turning over three or fooF 
iimes without disturbing the folds.

To Olban Silk. 

To Remove Grease Spots. — Scrape Venetian or French 
chalk fiae; moisten to a stiff paste with soap-suds; make ii 
into flat cakes by pressing between two boai'ds, and dry in 
the sun or oven. Keep these for use. When you need 
them, scrape one to powder and cover the spot with it, lay- 
ing the silk upon a fine clean linen or cotton cloth. Lay 
two or three folds of tissue-paper upon the ch.alk, and 
press it with a hot iron for a minute or more, taking care 
it does not touch the silk. Baise the paper and scrape otf 
the grease with the chalk. Split a visiting-card, and rub 
the place where the spot was, with the inside, to restore the 
l\istre. The silk should be pressed on the wrong side. 

If the spot be discovered at once, simply rub the wrong 
side hard with powdered French chalk, and leave it to weai 
off. 

To Wash SUk—Mxs. together 

2 cups cold water. 
1 tablespoonful honey, 
1 " soft soap. 

1 wineglass alcohol. 

Shake up well ; lay the silk, a breadth at a time, on a 
table, and sponge both sides with this, rubbing it well in ; 
shake it about well and up and down in a tub of cold water; 
flap it as dry as you can, but do not wring it. Hang it by 
the edges, not the middle, until fit to iron. Iron on the 
wrong side while it is very damp. 

Black and dark or sober-colored silks may be sucooBft- 
fully treated it this way. 

To Smooth Wrinkled Silk. — Sponge on the right aide 
mth very weak gum-arabic water, and iron on the wrtng 
side.

To Kenew Wbinkled Cbapk. 

Stretch over a basin of boiling water, holding it smooth, 

hut not tight, over the top, and shiftiug as the steam fairljj 

penetrates it. Fold, while damp, in the original creases^ 

and lay under a heavy book or board to dry. It wiU look 

. idiuost as well as new. 

To Kestoke the Pile op Velvet. 

If but slightly pressed, treat tis you would crape. Steam 
on the right side until heated through. If very badly 
crushed, wet on the wrong side ; let an assistant hold a hot 
iron, bottom upward, and pass the wet side of the velvet 
Blowly over the flat surface — a sort of upside-down ironing. 
'WTien the steam rises thickly through to the right side, it 
win raise the pile with it. Dry without handling. 

To CoRL Tumbled FEATnERS. 

Hold over the heated top of the range or stove, not near 
enough to burn ; withdraw, shake them out, and hold them 
over it again until curled. 

To Cleas Straw Matting. 

Wash with a cloth dipped in clean salt and water ; then 
wipe dry at once. This prevents it from turning yeUow. 

To Wash Lawn or Thik Muslin. 

Boil two quarts of wheat-bran in six quarts, or more, of 
water, half an hour. Strain through a coarse towel and 
tni-x in the water in which the muslin is to be washed. Use 
no soap, if you can help it, and no starch. Rinse lightly in 
fair water. This preparation both cleanses and stiffens the 
lawn. If you can tonveniently, take out all the gathers 
The skirt should always be ri[^)ped from the waist.

To Wash Wooljins. 

Wash in clean, hot soap suds ; rinse out in clear, hot 
* iter, and shake out the wet without passing through th« 
wringer. Worsted dress-goods should never be wrung when' 
washed. 

To Wash White, Lace Edging. 

Have a quart bottle covered with Hnen, stitched 
smoothly to fit the shape. Begin at the bottom and wind 
tlie lace about it, basting fast at both edges, even the 
minutest point, to the linen. Wash on the bottle, soaping 
^t well, rinse by plunging in a pail of fair water, and boil aa 
/ou would a white handkerchief, bottle and aU. Set iu the 
\iot sun to dry. When quite dry, clip the basting-threads, 
and use the lace without ironing. If neatly basted on, it 
•* iU look nearly as well as new — if not quite. 

Black Lace. 

^ cup rain water, or very soft spring water. 

1 teaspoonful borax. 

1 tablespoonful spirits of wine. 

SfjUeeze the tumbled rusty lace through this four times, 
then rinse in a cup of hot water in which a black kid glova 
has been boiled. Pull out the edges of the lace until almost 
dry ; then press for two days between the leaves of a heavy 
book. 

To Sponge Black Worsted Deesses. 

Sponge on the right side with a strong tea made of Jlff^ 
kaifea, and iron on the wrong. 

This process i-estores lustre and crispness to alpaca 
bombazine, etc.

To Eemovb Stains fkom Mabblb. 

Make a mortar of unslacked lime and very strong lyo, 
Cover the spot thickly with it and leave it on for six weeks. 
Wash it off perfectly clean, and rub ha/rd with a brush dip- 
ped in a lather of soap and water. Polish with a smooth, 
hard brush. 

Iron Monuo 

Is as nearly ineradicable as it is possible for stain to be. 
Try moistening the part injured with ink, and while this 
Is wet, nib in muriatic acid diluted with five times its 
weight of water, I have heard that the old and new stain 
can sometimes be removed together by this operation. 

Mildew 

Is likewise obstinate. If anything will extract it, it is 
lemon-juice mixed with an equal weight of salt, powdered 
starch, and soft soap. Hub on thickly and lay upon tlie 
grass in the hot sun ; renewing the application two or three 
(times a day until the spot fades or comes out. 

I have also used salt ■>»«■( with tomato-juice, often re- 
newed, laying the article s-'ained upon the grass. Some 
times the Btaii> was taken out, sometimes not.

Antidotes to Poison. 

For amy poison swallow instantly a glass of cold watei 
with a heaping teaspoonful of common salt and one of 
ground mustard stirred in. This is a speedy emetic. 
'"Vhen it has acted, swallow the whites of two raw eggs. 

If you have taken corrosive sublimate take half a 
liozen raw eggs besides the emetic. If laudanum, a cup 
of very strong coflfee. If arsenic, first the emetic, then 
balf a. cup of sweet oil or melted lard.

Habd Soap. 

6 lbs. washing soda. 
3 " unslaked lime. 

Pour on i gallons boiling water. 

Let it stand until perfectly clear, then drain oB, Fai 
in 6 lbs. clean fat. 

BoU until it begins to harden — about two hours — stir- 
ring most of the time. 

While boiling, thin with two gallons of cold water, 
which you have poured on the alkaline mixture after 
draining off the four gakons. ItMa must also settle clear 
before it is drawn off. Add it when there is danger of 
Jioiling over. 

Try the thickness by cooling a little on a plate. Put 
in a handfiU of salt just before taking from the fire. 
Wet a tub to prevent sticking ; turn in. the soap and let 
it stand until solid. Cut into bars ; put on a board and 
let it dry. 

This will make about forty pounds of nice soap ; much 
better for washing (when it has dried out for two or thre« 
months) than yellow turpentine soap.
